0143038583|4.0|I sit at a table in a McDonald's staring at my Dollar Menu Double Cheeseburger, it stares back at me. Deep within me I feel a problem, a twitch in my conscience asking me should I really be eating this? Then I ask my conscience, If not this than what is it I am supposed to be eating? I have the ability to eat almost any type of food their is, I am not faced with the strict diet of grass grazing or having to stalk my prey and feast of their raw flesh. I have the option of choice, the omnivore's choice of diversity in my diet, but what should I pick? This dilemma stirs all of us deep inside our psyche's, this dilemma is our dilemma, the omnivore's dilemma. In Michael Pollan's New York Times Bestseller, \\"The Omnivore's Dillema\\", you are confronted with many challenges as he deconstructs the industrial food web that we are all a part of and exposes the passe morals of the massive industry and how it affects out diets.         Commodity corn is the first subject Pollan covers in the first section titled, \\"Industrial: Corn\\". To do research for the topic Michael travels to Farnhamville, Iowa where he learns the ins and outs of the business of industrial farming with George Naylor. He learns what a massive impact corn has on agriculture and the food industry. Did you know that corn is in almost a fourth of everything in the grocery store? The massive amounts of byproducts that are made from corn come at a cheaper price than traditional goods and have grown to be integrated into a large sum of what we eat. Life in America would not be nearly as prosperous if corn had not been created through a \\"catastrophic sexual transmutation\\" from the teosinte grass variety. At one point in the book it even goes as far as including a quote that reads \\"Maize is the greatest gift god gave to man.\\" While this is a very tall statement many would find it odd it still does have some merit especially in the massive business of commodity corn we have raised to be today. How do we escape these pressure's? Some believe that organic farming process are the answer.          Organic thinking and morals is the subject matter that Michael details in the second section of the book titled \\"Pastoral: Grass.\\" The section begins with the look into the organic movement of such stores such as Whole Foods. With out having to look very deep he finds most of the major organic stores to be as much of a moral blending as he discovered in industrial corn farming. What it comes down to in any major industry is making money, and the true insight can easily be lost in our capitalist society. He moves his research to Polyface Farms in Virginia's Shenandoah Valley which is owned by Joel Salatin. Here he learns about the methods of what Joel calls \\"beyond organic\\" farming. These practices do in fact reach out far beyond the reaches or conventional organic farming and branch out to helping on a local and sustainable level. His farming methods are almost completely self contained except for the few outside products he must buy such as chicken feed, other than that all of his animals are used to do their natural processes in a manner that complete the necessary cycle to have a successful farm. In Joel's words, \\"The animals do all the work.\\" This is the part of the book that caught my attention the most. I liked it because it wasn't about the perils of an industry, such as the section breaking down the corn business, but instead it detailed the methods of a wholesome and logical method of living and creating life for others as well. This section really opened my eyes to ethics that farmers in our local area use and how they could be changed to create a more positive influence on the land they are utilizing to make profit.         My usual reading selections are normally very similar. I frequently go for books that will make me think on a worldly level outside of my own and focus on the philosophies behind things I have never personally experienced. This was not the case when I chose \\"The Omnivore's Dilemma\\", because the problems faced are happening in my own place in time. The moral issues are ones that most people face daily in our society because we all eat. But what are we really eating and what does it say about us? Michael Pollan would agree with the old saying, \\"You are what you eat.\\" I think these are questions we can all over look at times in out busy lives, but they apply to us all. I have a strong connection to the book and every other person in the world, especially in America, does too. This is why I recommend this book to anyone who eats. Which is all of you. Beyond the subject matters directness it is also simply a good and interesting book that will surprise you with its many quirky facts Pollan discovers as he travels deep into the heart of the food industry.         As that cheeseburger will stare at most of us, we will stare back, think, yet still eat it. Is it too late for the Omnivore? Will our practices one day burn themselves out? Our patterns we have created in the world are very heavily set into our culture and are very hard to change. We base our lives around these ways and practice them everyday. This is the way it has been and will remain for sometime until we the people make an effort to change it. Do we need to change our ways though? Who knows. I don't, you don't and neither does Michael Pollan. No one does. The way we live is just the way things are, someday it might have disastrous effects but at this point opinions on both sides can be written off as radical soapbox propaganda. For now, we do live in a place of little movement, but someday if that change comes it will be a vast departure from our living now.

1423113381|4.0|The Red Pyramid, by Rick Riordan, starts readers off on another series intermingling ancient mythology, today's world, and snappy young teens. In this case, though, the mythology is Egyptian, not Greek as in his Percy Jackson series (or Roman, as in the newest addition to that series) and the young teens aren't the sons/daughters of gods but are possessed by them (if that doesn't seem like much of a difference, it's  because it really isn't as the story plays out). This may seem overly familiar, but why mess with a good thing is probably Riordan's thinking, and my guess is his fans' as well.    And truth be told, there's a lot to like in Riordan's writing, especially for a YA audience. The books are fast-paced (admittedly sometimes too much so); the dialogue mostly smacks of real pre-teen and teen-speak, rather than that painfully obvious how an out-of-touch adults \\"thinks\\" teens speak; the characters are accessible and likable (even some of the \\"bad\\" guys); they tend to be funny (though this one's  humor is a bit forced at times); the lessons are solid; and hey, kids might actually learn something (but don't tell them that).    All of those pretty much hold true for The Red Pyramid as well. The novel pretty much opens (nearly so) with a bang. Literally, as the Rosetta Stone gets blown up in the British Museum by Carter and Sadie Kane's father, in what appears to be an attempt to bring their mother back to life. Instead, several Egyptian gods are freed, some take possession of various humans (or \\"host\\" in them), including Sadie and Carter, and the kids' father is taken. Soon, we're off to Brooklyn (having picked up the cat-goddess Bast, an old uncle, and a baboon), then to Washington D.C., then to Phoenix (with stops in several other locales, including the Land of the Dead) having encountered and/or picked up some magicians, various other gods and monsters, and a possible traitor or two, to stop the entombment of their father in a great red pyramid being built by the bad guy Set who wants to destroy the world, though he isn't really the \\"big bad.\\"  And that isn't close to all. And of course, it leaves room for the sequel.    The ride is pretty much nonstop and breathless. I wouldn't have minded some time to rest, but my guess is his audience will eat up the thrill ride. The plot lines are suspenseful: will they stop Set in time, will they find out who the traitor is, will they learn their powers, how will they deal w/ the gods inside of them, do they like or LikeLike their clear matches (well, the last one isn't really all that suspenseful). To be honest, I do tire a bit of the heroes' magic arriving or not arriving at opportune moments, and the way in which they can morph into power so easily is a bit thin (in Percy Jackson it's because they're demi-gods, here it's because they host gods and have the blood of pharoahs); what happened to learning and working for power?  But I'm the grumpy old man telling these kids to get off my lawn; again, I'm pretty sure the YA audience won't mind a bit.    The first-person narration switches between Carter and Sadie and both are winning voices, though they could have been a bit more distinctive. They have the trademark Riordan light patter down, but here Riordan has upped the emotional ante a bit, adding some depth to the characters (though he cheats a bit at the end I'd say. Did I mention I walked to school uphill both ways? Through snow?). The side characters, save for an ancient magician, are a bit flat, but as the focus is pretty much on the two kids throughout, that doesn't detract too much.    Riordan is one of those YA authors I'd say who doesn't have quite the cross-over appeal of some YA authors, lacking that richness of background detail or depth of character that is more appealing to an older audience. But he knows how to hit his target audience and hit them he does, again and again and again. The Red Pyramid is recommended for Percy fans and for YA readers, especially younger ones. Adults and older YA could still enjoy the book, but will probably find it a bit wanting.

B00192IV9U|3.0|Every artist that departs `much too soon' always leaves a vault filled with unreleased materials.  If anyone could give Tupac Shakur a run for his money, it is none other than Michael Jackson.  A creative mind flowing with so many ideas, Jackson's artistry could be captured by only Michael Jackson himself.  [[ASIN:B00192IV9U MICHAEL]] is the first of several planned posthumously released albums from the late, great pop star and while it does not showcase Jackson at his most prodigious, it does not embarrass or compromise his greatness as an artist.  There are enough solid numbers to make Michael an enjoyable, though not revolutionary addition to Michael Jackson's discography.<br /><br />\\"Hold My Hand,\\" the true promo single off of Michael featuring Akon (which should probably be billed Akon featuring Michael Jackson), features solid production work, but feels more like an Akon song as opposed to an MJ one. The main problem is there is too much Akon, which takes away from Jackson's voice, making people wonder how much extra polish Akon (also the producer) had to add to make this track `a go.'  Despite this, the track is more than presentable and smartly ends with Jackson taking the lead.  \\"Hollywood Tonight\\" opens up with Gregorian Chant at the beginning, then erupts into fierce production work with infuses pop, rock and urban influences. The track is a bit clumsy in sound, proving quickly not to be `first-rate' Michael Jackson.  That said, the cut is catchy and quite enjoyable, but also overstuffed with too many layers of background vocals.<br /><br />\\"Keep Your Head Up\\" is a better cut than its predecessors, finding the late king of pop at his best - tender balladry. Here, Jackson's falsetto is immaculate as is his total vocal performance.  The track drives on a bit too long, clocking in just under five minutes, but none can deny the effectiveness and emotion of the gospel chorus supporting Jackson.  It doesn't rival \\"Will You Be There\\" or \\"Earth Song\\" (both featured Andrae Crouch and his gospel chorus as support), but that is the closest comparison that can be asserted.  \\"(I Like) The Way You Love Me\\" opens with a recording of Jackson singing what he was looking for musically, which is a superb production touch by the producers.  Jackson sounds in top form here, and while the background vocals provide nice support, there are too many of them and they have too many added vocal effects.  The chorus is strong, finding Jackson singing: \\"I like the way how you love me/I like the way how you hold me/I like the way that you touch me/I like the way how you kiss me...\\" Overall, \\"(I Like) The Way You Love Me\\" proves to be a standout, despite some minor flaws.<br /><br />\\"Monster\\" featuring 50 Cent features unique and interesting production work, though it is not first-rate Jackson by any means. Some of the questions lie with what are explicit overdubs, when was this recorded, and how much did producers `ready' this cut for this release?  Questions aside, the hook (\\"Monster/he's a monster/ he's an animal\\") proves catchy, though 50 Cent's guest verse isn't particularly noteworthy or memorable, making you question if the MC has lost his spark given his most recent lackadaisical collaborations.  \\"Best Of Joy\\" is a bit undercooked, but enjoyable enough.  The length is appropriate at only 3:02, though the repetition makes the listener yearn for more substance and tighter songwriting.<br /><br />The controversial \\"Breaking News\\" begins with begins with the paparazzi/news media clips surrounding the controversies of Jackson; it reminds the listener of the material from HIStory or even Invincible. Again, the question is `when' were these gems recorded; what is their history? The production is nice, if dense, and the cut seems to be worthy of some spins.  The Lenny Kravitz feature \\"(I Can't Make It) Another Day\\" is stronger, though not necessarily a hit.  The rock edge to Michael Jackson's voice is nice to hear and the production work certainlystands out amongst the rest.<br /><br />\\"Behind the Mask\\" is one of the signature tunes from this effort, with nice driving, if the slightest bit `manic' production work.  This performance is older than most and perhaps thats why it sounds more `natural' and `nuanced.'  Closing cut \\"Much Too Soon\\" is hands down the best performance with stripped production work that suits Jackson well.  The background vocals work best here as they are subtle.<br /><br />Essentially, Michael is better than expected, but not a victory lap for the late singer.  Jackson's best work lies in the material that his mega audiences have come and continue to love.  That said, Michael is no shameful addition to the singer discography, and no Jackson fan can deny their eagerness to continue to support one of the greatest artists of our time..      \\"Behind the Mask\\" is one of the signature tunes from this effort, with nice driving, if the slightest bit `manic' production work. 1400067936|5.0|This is one of the best books I've read. A lot can be summarized as follows:  An astronomer looks out the window while riding in a train and after seeing a sheep says: Hey I didn't know all sheep in Ireland were black.  The physician next to him looks out and says: You cannot imply that, there are sheep in Ireland, and some of them are black.  They both look at the mathematician in front of them expecting a resolution. The mathematician briefly looks out and says: All I can say is that there is at least one sheep in Ireland and... half of it is black.    Taleb replaces the astronomer for traders, economists, politicians and, psychologists among others. And warns us about arriving to conclusions too fast. Randomness, he explains, should humble us and we should admit how little we know.  If you can get through the prickly arrogance of the author, you'll find a jewel in this book. If it had less of his personality and more of the thesis it'd be more enjoyable, but even as it is I fully recommend it. You may find the word \\"I\\" repeated constantly and would think it was written by your narcissist nephew, but if you can get over that, you'll discover serious thought experiments that don't require demonstration.    Taleb knows his subject, and he has the honesty that most traders and economists lack. We simply know very little, let's get over it. We act as if we knew what we were doing, but the results are more often than not random.  He illustrates how even basic elements of statistics such as expected value are ignored by \\"recognized\\" authorities in all fields.    A main thesis of the book, that risk, defined as the value of the outcome times its probability serves as a cautionary tale to all of us when evaluating possible courses of actions.  We warns us that even the more serious scientist, looking for confirmation of what we want to believe, fall into crass errors. All you need is enough scientists trying to prove something. Some of them will succeed. This reminds me of the physicist that wanted to prove that all odd numbers were prime as follows: 1 is prime, 3 is prime, 5 is prime, 7 is prime, 9 is... an experimental error, 11 is prime...    Bottom line: Buy this book, you won't regret it.

Films are the modern equivalent of classic stage plays and I'm sure Shakespeare himself would appreciate the power of film and what is possible with storytelling when using it as the medium.<br /><br />As someone who always struggled to stay interested in classics, despite the beautiful language used, I find a modern telling of a classic much more accessible than simple raw readings of his works which were meant to be seen as plays, not as words on a page. As time goes on the old language can get lost on the page, which does not have the luxury of a novels description. Quality is flat, in the good sense, and reproduces a mix accurately. It doesn't boost the bass like many modern cans are made to, so these are probably not what you want if most of your music is rap or hip-hop, where extra heavy bass is favored. These are great for a polished Languedoc LP mix and especially good for those extra crunchy AUDs you're pulling from etree.<br /><br />-Comfort. The pads they come with are nice and soft although I found that my ears got hot and slightly sweaty after some time with them on. You'll see a lot of heads recommending that you get a pair of beyourdynamic velour pads to slap onto these puppies, and let me tell, they are right. Definitely do it. I was reluctant to spend an extra $20 on those pads since I was saving that money for some extra heady lot crystals and maybe a veggie burrito but the trade-off was worth it in the end.<br /><br />-Durability. I've only had these for a month, but when I was changing the earpads, I didn't realize the foam comes off by itself, and I attempted to pull off the entire cover of the earcup. That's what you get when changing earpads with a head full. ANYWAYS it had just finished raining outside and the lucky charms dude slid down a rainbow and told me I just have to roll off the old pads and than slip on the new ones. Then he rode off into the sunset with Ernie and Bert while they all raged a sick Reba jam. So I changed the pads, and even with my aggresive pulling, my cans are still in fine shape. I'm not worried about these suckers breaking on me anytime soon.<br /><br />Quick recap: Comfort is very good, I would reccomend upgrading the padding to beyerdynamic velour (make sure right model) if you're ears get sweaty.<br />Durability seems to be great.<br />Sound is great for those uber heady Phish AUDs, your Beatles Remasters, and more, but not so much for hip hop and rap. Sampled some classical music which also played through nicely. I did think my Airto Momeira sounded funny though (latin jazz)

We want to accomplish great things and receive great honors in life, but many times we just want to get there without doing a whole lot of work or going through personal transformations that are necessary with each of life's journeys. The following dialogue portrays this tendency:    \\"Why are you called the Alchemist?\\"  \\"Because that's what I am.\\"   \\"And what went wrong when other alchemists tried to make gold and were unable to do so?\\"   \\"They were only looking for gold,\\" his companion answered.   \\"They were seeking the treasure of their Personal Legend, without wanting actually to live out the Personal Legend.\\"     The Alchemist discovered his own Personal Legend of discovering how to turn any metal into gold, and drinking from the elixir of life. B000V9D5LG|4.0|The Canon EF-S 55-250mm is a perfect choice if you shoot mostly wide angle shots and don't want to blow a ton of money on a telephoto zoom lens that you only use occasionally. This lens offers you reasonably sharp photos at a very, very good price. Here's a more in-depth evaluation of this lens.<br /><br />- Sharpness<br />The sharpness is on par with that of the 18-55mm kit lens. Although the lens is not quite pro-grade, it can, in the right conditions, impress even an avid pixel-peeper. For a budget lens, it's really about as sharp as they come. This lens will probably satisfy all but the most fastidious of prosumers.<br /><br />Hey, don't take my word for it; seek out professional review sites that show you a 100% crop of pictures taken with this lens and then decide for yourself.<br /><br />- Chromatic Aberration (CA)<br />In bright sunlight, where chromatic aberration is most pronounced, the chromatic aberration on this lens is barely noticeable and probably imperceptible to the untrained eye. In more even lighting, this lens shows no signs of CA.<br /><br />- Auto-Focus (AF)<br />The auto-focus is fast enough for still subjects, but for very fast-moving subjects, it's a hit-or-miss. I gave this lens a good workout shooting cheetahs running close to their top speeds at the Animal Ark, Reno, NV (OT: cheetahs can run at speeds in excess of 70 mph). Most of my shots came out blurry (and that's not due to motion blur, mind you) because the auto-focus wasn't fast enough.<br /><br />Like the kit lens, the auto-focus has trouble in lowlight conditions. It also has trouble with surfaces that lack texture. The motor whirs back and forth trying to lock on to a focus. It can be a little annoying at times!<br /><br />- Image Stabilization (IS)<br />The image stabilization is amazing, even at 250mm. Interestingly, you can hear a click when the image stabilization mechanism kicks in. For handheld shots, especially at the 250mm end, the IS is immensely helpful for steadying your shots. I'm very impressed. (** Bear in mind, too, the rule of thumb: the shutter speed should be at least as fast as the reciprocal of the focal length. **)<br /><br />- Internal Focus (IF)<br />The lack of IF would not ordinarily be a problem unless you're using circular polarized filters. The turning of the barrel throws the filter out of adjustment, so you would have to readjust the filter after the subject is brought into focus.<br /><br />- Build<br />In terms of build quality, the lens is built a lot like the kit lens. There are complaints that it looks plasticky - maybe so - but it doesn't look cheap to be sure. It's lightweight but it feels well-built and sturdy. The zoom ring feels tight, but not overly so, so there is no zoom creep.<br /><br />- Alternative<br />Just FYI, the [[ASIN:B0007Y794O Canon EF 70-300mm]] is reputed to be sharper than this lens.
